B001O8C5EQ|5.0|It's hard for me to choose a favorite Porcupine Tree album. They're hard to compare because of the way the band evolved over the years and they all have at least one weak track that has to be taken into account and compared to the better songs. After much listening, however, I've decided that 1996's \\"Signify\\" is their highest achievement. Out of all their releases, it works the best as a singular piece of work, that is, an album rather than a collection of songs. I don't own it on vinyl, but I bet it's got one of those sicknasty gatefold sleeves that were big in the 70's.  This music exists in its own world, a trait it has in common with classic albums by Pink Floyd and Radiohead. The production invokes images of a place that's mostly black void, with objects and scenes forming and dissolving into and out of the darkness. A nightmare world, perhaps. There's certainly enough creepy to go around for it to be one. There is a handful of relativel conventional songs on the album (\\"Sleep of No Dreaming\\" and \\"Every Home Is Wired\\" come to mind), but much of \\"Signify\\" is devoted to soundscapes that, while not entirely abstract, aren't exactly grounded in reality either. These songs are fascinating because they take elements from 90's techno/electronic music and give them a progtastic modification by using rock instruments like electric guitar, organ and live drums. \\"Idiot Prayer\\", for example, is pretty much one-of-a-kind, with its angry, sharp guitars and oddly dance floor-friendly bass line. There are samples of a man who seems to be trying to describe a DMT trip. He has a short monologue at one point, and one snippet of him saying \\"Please help\\" that repeats throughout the frantic parts.  My favorite track here is \\"Sever\\", a song that I suspect is meant to be what insanity sounds like. There's a flock of garbled voices in the mix, which to me suggests that the narrator is schizophrenic. I've read the lyrics, and they don't appear to have a discernible meaning, at least notto anyone other than the narrator. Perhaps you've met someone like this, whose mental illness causes confusion in their thoughts and words. They're speaking almost nonsensically, but you think that there might be some kind of message they have that they can't articulate. A similar thing may happen to a person who's taken a large dose of a hallucinogen. Even the song's vocal melody has a not-quite-right feel to it. I find it hard to decide exactly what emotion it's conveying. Fear? Anxiety? A kind of demented exuberance? Sadness? It's really hard to tell.  Listen to this album in the dark.

B005ELQVGW|2.0|Like a few reviewers here, I am a serious Bjork enthusiast who has enjoyed collecting her work and seeing her perform live for many years.  Much of her work has been a backdrop to important incidents in my life.  \\"Vespertine\\" is one of the greatest albums I've ever heard.<br /><br />I was really looking forward to the release of \\"Biophilia\\".  I had enjoyed the album \\"Volta\\" quite a lot - I thought there were some great songs there and some incredible instrumentation.  When I heard the first track of \\"Biophilia\\" (\\"Moon\\") I smiled to hear that lovely sound of the harp, one of Bjork's signature instruments.  \\"Moon\\" is a lovely song, with some lyrical moments that recall some of the most heart-felt Bjork phrases.  When she sings \\"Fail at loving, Fail at creating a flow...\\" your emotions nearly boil over.<br /><br />Yet this is the high water mark of the album.  As the rest of the tracks emerge, I have to say I was really stunned by how boring they were.  I had read review after review praising this album and I started to wonder if I'd actually bought the wrong version.  The arrangements of these tuneless melodies is so sparse and spare that there are huge gaps (almost like dead air) continually popping up in the songs.  \\"Thunderbolt\\" is a perfect example.  Academically, on paper, the song sounds fascinating.  But Bjork's usual finesse with lyrics in English is embarrassingly absent.  After she repeats the phrase 'Craving miracles' for about the fifth time, I'm cringing with how new-age and feel-good it all sounds.<br /><br />For all the hype about the various instruments created to be played on the album, the eventual sound is not fascinating by any stretch.  The combination piano/Javanese gamelon used in \\"Chrystaline\\" could just as easily have been created with a sampler.  This, along with the strange assertions that the album is a celebration of nature and science, doesn't become more than the sum of the parts.  Tossing a few lyrics about dirt, crystals and rocks into a song doesn't make it scientific... in fact, the opposite happens; it feels pedantic and cheesy.<br /><br />Lyrically, the album seems to make tons of references to a thread-bare metaphor of the earth / the body.  Vertebrae are rocks, etc.  This is an interesting area to explore, but Bjork has done this already in her previous albums.  This idea, being heralded as revolutionary, has been a consistent theme of painters, writers and musicians for hundreds of years.  She's not adding anything new to the idea.  Of course it isn't Bjork's responsibility to break new ground with each new album, but with all the hyped elements of \\"Biophilia\\", anyone expecting her usual quirkiness will instead be met with that really bad side affect of innovation: Pretentiousness.  For example, Bjork is at the point in her career in which she thinks she can carry a song only accompanied by a solo instrument.  This is the case with the song \\"Solstice\\", the closing track of the album, which is almost painful to listen to.  It is another tuneless track full of recycled Bjork's usual musical phrasing.  Her songwriting \\"style\\" overtakes her actual songwriting.  It's hard to end on a note like this.<br /><br />Bjork is also beginning to suffer from a fate similar to Tori Amos - she can't seem to stop playing \\"dress-up\\" (for lack of a better phrase.)  I think Bjork wants to be taken very seriously as an artist, and yet she's still donning tons and tons of make-up and the trappings of a community theater experimental dance troupe.  If she really doesn't care about such things, why all the theatrics?  The artifice?  For once I'd like to see her perform in jeans and a t-shirt and just be herself.  Perhaps she's finding it difficult to age gracefully.  If her work for \\"Biophilia\\" was strong enough, there would be no need for such extensive visualization.  I won't even start on the whole multi-format release issue.  Bottom line: No app is going to make a poor song sound any better.<br /><br />I had genuine interest in this album before its release. Since I've bought it, I've listened to it three times, and I've got no impetus to put it on for another listen.  There's nothing waiting there for me.  I can only hope that Bjork has a few more good albums inside her that she'll bring forth in the future.  Unfortunately, at the rate she's been releasing work, it will be many years before that happens.enuine interest in this album before its release. Since I've bought it, I've listened to it three times, and I've got no impetus to put it on for another listen.  There's nothing waiting there for me.  I can only hope that Bjork has a few more good albums inside her that she'll bring forth in the future.  Unfortunately, at the rate she's been releasing work, it will be many years before that happens.
B00000ILDE|3.0|The original Alien was a ground breaking science fiction that relied on mood and atmosphere to achieve its scares. Ridley Scott was smart not to show too much of the Alien, instead leaving its menace up to the viewer's imagination. The alien spacecraft is a masterful piece of design concocted by H. R. Giger, and looks appropriately otherworldly lurking in the ominous background of the alien landscape.  However, James Cameron took a different approach with the sequel Aliens. Like its predecessor the film is a triumph of industry design and technology. However, Cameron unlike Scott seems to be more fascinated in action and military hardware. This does not say that they are scares or thrills in the sequel, but as the movie moves along there is a lot of gunplay between the soldiers and the aliens. The film starts to look like one of those shooting games you play on the internet. Initially when you see the first alien it is quite a shock, but after hundreds have been blown away it gets monotonous. Many of today's action fantasies owe a lot to the pacing of aliens.  The most impressive thing about Aliens is the confrontation Ripley has with the queen alien. It is one of the last full sized mechanized monsters shown well before the CGI revolution. Its movements are impressive considering it was probably moved with electric motors. The final battle is impressively mounted and the Alien is dispatched.  To be fair maybe one should look at Alien and Aliens as two different films done by directors with contrasting styles. I prefer the more subtle approach of Scott, than Cameron's more action orientated direction. That said, it looks as if both films are already classics.

0786939532|5.0|When I think of The Legend of Drizzt by R.A. Salvatore, I think about many things. I think of all the deep and varied characters, all thoughtfully and creatively developed. I think of the amazing combative description, with soaring mithral scimitars and shrieking silver arrows. I also think of the vast and complex journey which connects all of this, and the world in which it takes place, from the frigid Spine of the World Mountains to the crowded streets of southern Calimport. But most of all I think of the hero, Drizzt Do'Urden (short for Drizzt Do'Urden Daermon'Nashezbaernon), and his eternal quest to escape the legacy of his heritage and discover the inner truths of the world. It is his quest, along with those of all he meets, that truly draw me to this series.<br /><br />  So first, I will discuss all of the characters that shape this tale and give it depth. One of the most interesting things about this series is the fact that characters, even minor sub-villains or characters that only appear for a single chapter or book, give the impression of realism often lacking in fantasy novels. When backstory isn't vital, it is still given or at least hinted at. While one might think that this could quickly make stories plodding and dull, the descriptions never overstay their welcome and are always intriguing. The story quickly and frequently switches between characters, creating multifaceted tales which intertwine in clever and often unexpected ways.  However, just as many times a character isn't an integral part of the main tale, and that allows for a background world that doesn't feel completely centered on the goals and aspirations of the major characters.<br /><br />  The combat is another one of the main draws to this series. Salvatore's style of portraying combat can convey all manners of emotion, from quick, rowdy tavern brawls to solemn and precise displays of swordsmanship, from high-intensity fights to the death to lighthearted and daring games of sparring. Theintense visceral action of broken noses, gashed arms, and even spines ripped straight from backs, adds an intensity and level of description most writers hesitate to explore. Detailed descriptions of individual combat maneuvers bring visualization to new levels, and inspire awe and respect of the grace and finesse (or raw power) of the combatants. The combat is also made more urgent, as Salvatore avoids the common pitfall of easy and readily accessible healing magic, which tends, in turn, to lead to less detailed and interesting battles. Besides, what's the point of detailing what happens if a quick incantation can nullify most injuries? Finally, I'd like to mention that the victories and defeats of this series are determined by the D&D combat system; the entire plot could change with one lucky (or unlucky!) roll of the dice, and Salvatore would have to cope. So for instance, if it weren't for one extremely improbable throw of a sword, the main (human) villain, the assassin Artemis Entreri might never have entered the Drow city of Menzzoberanzan. Salvatore then took all the statistical data and beautifully translated it into realistic and intriguing depictions of each and every adventure. The final point I want to touch up on is the magnificent world Salvatore Depicts. Though Salvatore himself didn't create the vast and varied realm of Faerun, he had to try to paint it as it was hundreds of years before it was created. Of course, some of this history was already in place, yet not so for the forgotten kingdoms, tiny fiefdoms, and ancient governments all of which were created by Salvatore's own hand. Places interact as surely as people, and everywhere in the entire world has its own flavor. Descriptions which paint the harsh, barren wastelands like Icewind Dale chill the bones; grand depictions of the silvery Mithral Hall, with colossal, raging furnaces to the brilliant rivers of ore flowing through the walls. Each location seems to have a life of its own, independent from the main story, as guilds rise and fall and powerful individuals struggle for power. All of these aspects combine to create an incredibly organic and fluid world, which feels much less mechanical than your average fantasy.<br /><br />  This series does a wonderful job of connecting you to the characters, but not making them seem overbearing in the grand scheme of things. The writing style makes for a multi-leveled and complex story, with plenty of great description and an appropriate amount of twists. Although the stories are full of subplots, they never dilute the main intent of the story. It takes serious looks at many themes and issues, including racism and innocence to experience. It's a fun and interesting read that has credible (yet unobtrusive) morals. Drizzt's adventures are beloved by many fans of D&D, and, in my opinion, should be considered a masterpiece of fantasy literature.

I didn't rush out to read the book though, just put it on my wish list and left it at that. Then, I saw the trailer for the film adaptation starring Jennifer Lawrence and Woody Harrelson, and that's when I realized I needed to get it over with and read this book. But after years of procrastination on my part and ceaseless hype on the part of those who published it, did it live up to my expectations?<br /><br />Set sometime in the future, civilization as we know has collapsed and in its place a new power structure has risen in Panem (what used to be North America), presiding over twelve colonies that all serve the Capitol. There used to be thirteen, but one city was destroyed during a civil war, and serves as a cautionary tale to the remaining twelve. Since peace was reestablished, an annual event airs on television. It's called the Hunger Games. Think of it as a macabre cross between American Idol and Stephen King's Running Man. And our protagonist, Katniss, a sixteen-year-old girl has just volunteered to enter, thus sparing her twelve-year-old sister from having to compete.<br /><br />Each of the twelve colonies randomly select one boy and one girl, between the ages of twelve and eighteen. After being whisked from their homes to the Capitol, they are lavished with every luxury imaginable during their training until they are all thrust into a remote landscape and forced to fight each other to the death until only one remains. Sounds like good watchin'. And it better be, because it's compulsory viewing for the colonists, as a reminder that rebellion will only serve to kill all their sons and daughters.<br /><br />For Katniss, the ideals of surviving to bring honor and glory, however fleeting, back home are a distant second to her longing to spare her mother and sister from seeing her weak and scared. She's spent much of her life providing for them in the wake of her father's death in a coal mine, and has become resilient and calloused in the process. In fact, aside from the selfless act of replacing her sister after the random drawing, Katniss isn't a terribly likable character. She's resentful towards her mother who had a mental breakdown after her Dad died, and is self-centered to a degree that's really irritating. Granted, her living conditions don't exactly lend themselves to a happy-go-lucky adolescence.<br /><br />The first half of the book spends time establishing who Katniss is and how she sees those around her, as she's plucked from her life and introduced to an Oz-like world in the Capitol. Peeta, the baker's son, is the other teen chosen to compete, and together they try to maneuver their way through ridiculous protocol and the stacked odds against them in training. As well, Katniss must weight how much she can trust Peeta once the games begin, because only one can be declared winner, and her paranoia has her questioning not only his every move, but her own as well. The second half of the book is all about the Hunger Games, when she, Peeta, and the twenty-two other teens are sent into the arena to fight. Here, things are really turned on their head, as Katniss finally realizes her hunting skills are the only thing that's going to keep her alive, namely her prowess with a bow and arrow. Unfortunately, the weapons are laid out as bait at the start of the games to encourage immediate confrontation, so Katniss has to settle for grabbing what supplies she can and hiding in the forest before one of the others kills her.<br /><br />The book is exciting for nearly the entire way through, with each chapter offering enough suspense and cliffhangers to make you keep turning pages. There are some aggravating aspects to the book, though. One of the big problems I had with the book was Katniss' evasion of killing others to survive. It seemed at nearly every turn, when herhand was forced and she had to defend herself, there was some kind of godhand stepping in to take care of the killing for her. I don't want to go into detail more than that, as that's spoiler enough I'm sure, but when other competitors are getting blood on their hands, Katniss seems to be surviving less by skill than by sheer luck. Another facet of the Hunger Games I didn't like was the idea of sponsors in the Capitol airdropping supplies to competitors at certain times during the games. Since Katniss and everyone else are being filmed by hidden cameras and tracking devices, their performances are used to garner sponsors for things like food or medicine or whatever the case may be. For Katniss, the little items that parachute their way were literal deus ex machina. Things are at their worst? Don't worry, here's that thing you desperately need and would otherwise die without it.<br /><br />That much I can ignore, but for me, the ending was just awful. This, I can't spoil, so sufficed to say there is something that happens towards the end of the Hunger Games that comes out of nowhere and just derails the whole story for me. And then--and I don't think I'm spoiling anything since this is the first book in a trilogy--after Katniss gets out of the Games, there is further annoyance to the \\"after show\\" and her attitudes towards other characters when things had settled down. I'm still looking forward to reading the second book, Catching Fire, but only because I'm hoping Suzanne Collins' plans for this story make up for the loose strings left in this book.re was some kind of godhand stepping in to take care of the killing for her. I don't want to go into detail more than that, as that's spoiler enough I'm sure, but when other competitors are getting blood on their hands, Katniss seems to be surviving less by skill than by sheer luck. Another facet of the Hunger Games I didn't like was the idea of sponsors in the Capitol airdropping supplies to competitors at certain times during the games. B0052FG8KO|5.0|In Progress is absolutely the best hard rock album of 2011, of that there is no doubt.  If you are even remotely a fan of this style of uplifting, wind through your hair, and rocking music, then this is a must have.  Utilizing soaring vocals that are also expertly utilized as a harmony tool, a lefty guitarist that not only looks similar to Paul Gilbert he is of the same high caliber, layers of lush keys, and insanely sharp hooks, these twelve songs rock you up and down California.  And the funny part about that?  These guys hail from Sweden.  Even though melodies run rampant this is a guitar driven and hard rocking album.  Guitar pyrotechnics rival Eddie Van Halen with fluid lead solo runs and driving rhythms.  The majority of the twelve songs here are definite up tempo and foot stompers for sure.  These guys succeed their peers Journey in the sense that they limit the ballads to a few bombshells of melodic C4 rather than pollute an album with drippy cheese.  Opener The Rain sets the bar at an insanely high level and sets the pace for things to come.  This sums up everything that is great about this style of music and has to be heard to be understood.  Its weather related cousin, Eye Of The Storm, is a perfect example of the deftly executed harmony vocals the band employs.  Sorta like an old school doo-wop act, the backing vocals weave in and harmonize with the main vocal line often during the verses and choruses respectively.  There are harmonies everywhere on this album.  Castaway, near album's end, has the largest, most gigantic chorus of not only the album, but probably this genre.  Starting off with a backwoods-like acoustic guitar and jaunty beat, this then flirts with a detuned modern riff before launching into a grandiose symphony of harmony vocals in the chorus.  Once listened to, it's no surprise why this album has been raved about by everyone that put pen to paper about it.  For fans of melodic rock and AOR, this album is on par with Bonfire's \\"Point Blank,\\" Jaded Heart's \\"Trust,\\" Axxis' \\"II,\\" and Balance Of Power's \\"Ten More Tales Of Grand Illusion,\\" and should be a blind buy. Amazon has probably scared sales because of that disclaimer but it is not true.  Get it quick.  It's now out of print.

But it is the welcoming embrace of a dream job offer which really locates herself.<br /><br />A man who finds sex at any 'oasis' is battling with a man who wants to remain in a monogamous relationship; his wife gives him some rope then reins it in when he asks for too much.<br /><br />Meanwhile a father figure is unrepentant in his belief that the sexual urge is behind everything. He has found a new land even though he does not yet know what lies ahead. He is the solid rock denying the chance for more intuitive reasoning to bloom. He is a man of science.<br /><br />A Dangerous Method should be viewed and then reflected upon. Responsibly.

B0064NTZJO|5.0|It's somewhat understandable that many viewers do not recognize when a movie pays homage to older films, because chances are the last films you saw were recent ones and not older ones.<br /><br />This is the main crime of those who developed a distaste for this film. The criticism falls into 2 main categories and 2 lesser categories, which I will address one at a time:<br /><br />- Silence / lack of dialogue: People are judging the acting based on the dialogue having awkward pauses, because this is not a dramatic tool that is common in the average modern \\"chatterbox\\" film, but it's clearly present in nearly all of Clint Eastwood's films. Even in \\"Grand Torino\\" it was important that he be stoic and not be too talkative. Modern films would lead us to believe there are no quiet characters in the real world. If you are a restless person who cannot stand the \\"silent type,\\" then please avoid watching this film or dropping negative reviews about it. It pays homage to Clint Eastwood, but also to Marlon Brando. \\"Apocalypse Now\\" was a very poetic film that really needed every single awkward silence to build the tension of it's poetics. \\"Drive\\" also pays homage to the silent villain of the original Halloween (especially in the scene when he's standing on the hill overlooking the beach), an homage that also carries-over into the next category.<br /><br />- The gore: I am not a fan of gory films, and I would have liked this film better with four less stomps to the head, so I'm shocked that one reviewer said Goslin's character was more teddy bear than psycho. Again, I do not like gore at all, and I still give this film a strong five stars. The gore is done in a style that I hesitate to call a grind-house homage simply because it's not the Kill Bill style where blood squirts halfway across the room, but it's also not shot in the slick resolution style of Final Destination. The gore style is more like Halloween than Evil Dead, but it has that dry dead-pan camera angle that never looks away when things get rough. The presence of violence is helpful to the story somewhat like \\"There Will Be Blood,\\" in order to give the impression that this guy has either been corrupted by circumstance, or secretly had a hidden psycho in him. Also, it counterbalances the idea that he is supposedly heartfelt and fighting for someone.<br /><br />Now for the lesser concerns:<br /><br />- Not enough driving: Are you seriously complaining about this? Do people really watch movies just because they have a lot of driving? I guess so. Then please by all means skip this and rent \\"Gone in 60 Seconds\\" (more driving than there is plot) or \\"Furious Five\\" (I hear it is the best driving movie, which is why it doesn't interest me). This movie starts and ends with driving and that leaves out at least three chase scenes, but I guess people wanted the killing to happen in cars, the romance to happen in cars, etc. Get over it. It's like complaining because people have sex in a movie called \\"The Opposite of Sex.\\"<br /><br />- The music: I guess if the music is what drove me to this movie in the first place, then it's only logical that it would be the factor that drove some people away. I would complain that it could use even more corny 80's-type synthesizer music. The actual soundtrack only has like six songs or so and the rest is just the composer's score of mood-evoking tones and what not. I would have preferred if Dust Brothers had handled the composer parts to give it more grit like they did on Fight Club, but it works regardless. The music goes with the \\"Risky Business\\" font used in the title credits and it goes with the character's jacket and gloves.<br /><br />Closing remarks: This is not really the \\"crossover film\\" it was portrayed to be, it's still mostly an arthouse type of film, but the cameras are not shaky, the violence is not cartoonish like Tarantino's style, and the scenes are not shot through dirty windows and stuff like my dad hates. This is a film that builds on the lineage of gangster films, suspense-type film noir, etc. It's only on the edge of the driving movie genre, but a film that the males in my family can all get behind, because it ties back to Reservoir Dogs, Scarface, and The Godfather. This is nothing like the high-pace/soft-violence of \\"Gone in 60 seconds.\\"he lineage of gangster films, suspense-type film noir, etc. It's only on the edge of the driving movie genre, but a film that the males in my family can all get behind, because it ties back to Reservoir Dogs, Scarface, and The Godfather. Kerpen uses tons of real world examples - from his company, and from social media fame/infamy - he shows a very strong understanding of people (as opposed to some of the very tech/market heavy books out there) and you can see that it comes from a background with his very successful company Likeable Media.  Lots of strong theory, and great exercises to help adapt his principles to your project.  I absolutely recommend this book.  And He totally tweets back.

B006Z7XNVK|5.0|It is difficult to watch this completely absorbing film MARTHA MARCY MAY MARLENE without knowing something about the creator Sean Durkin.  Even though he is still very young he presents evidence of an enormous talent. He has been quoted else where with the following statements: ``I think as a child I was really afraid of groups that conformed. Cults were really an example of that. I'm attracted to fear. I'm attracted to movies that scare you. I knew I would just end up working in that realm.' 'How can you make a movie about getting out of a cult if you've never gotten out of a cult?' Because movies are made by people who don't have to go through things. You have to understand that. I have to understand, emotionally and psychologically, what she went through. And I did that, then I tried to be true to that when I was making the movie. To understand sisters, I need to understand family relationships. I don't think of it in terms of male-female or having to live through it.<br /><br />With that information the plot line of this film seems more accessible, though it is a film that will appeal to a somewhat limited audience. It is an engrossing dissection of the mind of a girl whose life is so fractured that she escapes `ordinary life' to join a cult living on a farm where all of the members work and all are directed by the mindset of a quietly deranged man.<br /><br />Martha (Elizabeth Olsen in a knockout performance) has been living with a `family' headed by the charismatic, dark Patrick (John Hawkes) who has renamed her Marcy May.  Together with Zoe (Louisa Krause), Katie (Mary Dizia), Sarah (Julia Garner) - each of whom is bedded by Patrick in a ceremony of sorts - and with the male members of the family Max (Christopher Abbot) and Watts (Brady Corbett). When we first meet Marlene she is leaving the family, headed for sanctuary with her sister Lucy (Sarah Paulson) and brother-in-law Ted (Hugh Dancy) - two successful young people who are part of the `establishment'. Martha attempts to find peace with Lucy and Ted, but her nightmares and haunting paranoia make her appear like a severely mentally injured girl: the reasons for her behavior we discover through a series of playbacks of her strange and ultimately criminal life with the family.  Rather than bringing resolution to the film's story writer/director Durkin leaves Martha (now in her new role as Marlene) at a juncture where we the audience must decide the ending. It is this slowing unwinding of a girl's mind as displayed in the relationship with her nurturing but confused sister that makes one of the most thrilling psychological dramas in years.<br /><br />Even though the film as we watch it seems to be the career making performance of Elizabeth Olsen it is far more staggering a `first outing' for Sean Durkin.  From the setting in the Catskills and in Connecticut to the finely shaped performances by Sarah Paulson, Hugh Dancy, John Hawkes, Brady Corbett, and literally every member of this cast, this is one of the finest films of the year. 0439023513|4.0|Suzanne Collins' \\"The Hunger Games\\" trilogy concludes with \\"Mockingjay,\\" and our dystopian future has never been so bleak.  Similar to Stephen King's magnificent \\"The Long Walk,\\" a good story set in a world that kills children for our entertainment cannot have a happy ending.  \\"Mockingjay\\" opens with Katniss Everdeen recovering from her second triumph in the Hunger Games.  Only this time, she cannot look forward to a peaceful return home where her biggest burden will be the choice between her suitors, Gale and Peeta. As the Mockingjay, she is the symbol of the rebellion against the Capitol, led by the armies of District 13 and their hard-boiled leader, Coin.  Not only must Katniss accept the fact that virtually everyone lied to her about the planned rebellion, she must also confront a new reality where the rebels must consider some of the same hard choices that the leaders of the Capitol made.  In a bleak trilogy, \\"Mockingjay\\" stands out for being particularly harsh and violent.  The rebels may be suiting up for war, but Katniss and the other tributes who survived the Quarter Quell are mere shells of themselves.  Peeta, her devoted ally since the first Games, is in the clutches of President Snow in the Capitol and is surely the focus of their most dastradly tortures.  Gale, Kat's sidekick from District 12, has had virtually every ounce of pity boiled from his soul by war.  And Haymitch cannot mentor Kat any longer as they are on entirely new ground.  Even fans of the first two novels, violent in their own right, will be shocked by the unremitting violence Collins unleashes in \\"Mockingjay\\" as the rebels take the fight to the Capitol.  This is not a criticism - Collins does not want to fall back on the cliche of the rebels mounting a noble war against their oppressors.  Indeed, much of the book revolves around Kat's struggle to reconcile with her new allies.  Trust is just a word in \\"Mockingjay,\\" as empty as air.  This is a powerful, dark book that caps off the HungerGames surprisingly well.  If you know a younger reader who devoured the first two books, you might want to take a spin through this one before letting them take it on - kind of like how \\"The Deathly Hallows\\" required a more mature reader than \\"The Sorcerer's Stone,\\" and not just because it was longer.  Just be careful - this is a book that can cause nightmares.  Highly recommended.

0385534639|5.0|The Night Circus is well trodden territory here so I'll spare you the recap and skip to right to why this is a 5-star book.<br /><br />  1. The Circus itself. Dark, magical, and slightly menacing. As the sign says, \\"Trespassers Will Be Exsanguinated.\\" You believe it but willingly go regardless.<br />  2. The secrets are kept secret. Magic fades under harsh light and the author knows how to keep things bright by keeping the in the dark.<br />  3. The characters aren't explained so much as implied. For example,  Mr. A. H-- is a man so mysterious that we are never to know his name nor why he no longer casts a shadow. Yet we know that the Mr. A will stab you in the back and walk on without a second thought.<br />  4. The world that is created in this book is one of the most original and vibrantly imagined that I have read in years.<br />  5. The writing borders on poetry without weighing down the plot.<br />  6. The romance is darkly passionate and free of cliche.<br /><br />  \\"Marco kisses her as though they are the only two people in the world. The air swirls in a tempest around the, blowing open the glass doors to the garden with a tangle of billowing curtains. Every eye in the crowded ballroom turns in their direction. And the he releases her and walks away.\\"<br /><br />There are some small flaws in this book and I can understand why some would give it less than 5 stars. Some might not appreciate the fact that many of the mysteries are kept mysteries. Some might feel the characters are too distant and unrelatable. Or the ending too abrupt.<br /><br />But I assert that Erin Morgenstern is a major new talent and her inaugural work is a staggering work of beautiful fiction.

0525421580|4.0|What I liked: This book is a blast. It's a funny, loud, cute page-turner. I finished it in two days. The friendship between John Green's Will Grayson and his best friend, Tiny Cooper, is very sweet and realistic. His half of the book is endearing and insightful, funny and sad, brilliant and ridiculous.  What I didn't like: This novel is in first person, present tense, switches between the POVs of the two Will Graysons, and has two different authors. Basically, it has all of the major things that tend to make a book exceedingly bad. I definitely give them points (okay, mostly John Green) for pulling this off well enough that it was still a good read, but it could have been a great one.  Green and Levithan chose three of the hardest things to pull off in a novel and, unsurprisingly, their book suffered:  1. You need to be a fantastic author to pull off first person without self-insertion, contrivance or clich. You need to be an even better author to write fiction in present tense without annoying your reader or making tense change mistakes. These guys aren't fantastic. Judging solely from this book, David Levithan is fine and John Green is pretty good.  2. When you write a book from the POV of more than one character, every reader is bound to have a favorite and character development is extremely difficult. In the hands of an author who is less than genius, this always results in readers dreading certain parts and wishing others were longer. This technique also tends to make books feel disjointed and rushed. That's exactly what happened when I read this book. I disliked David Levithan's Will Grayson for the majority of the book. By the time I started to like him a little, it was too late. I loved John Green's Will Grayson, but I felt cheated out of a lot of time with him and found myself literally counting down the pages left before he was coming back.  3. The issues described in the previous point are exacerbated by the fact that the book has two different authors.It's tough for two people to collaborate and write an awesome, cohesive book, but it has been done. (See [[ASIN:0590213113 P.S. Longer Letter Later]] and its sequel for examples of this aimed at a younger audience.) While I was reading, I felt that the authors may not have spent enough time working on the book as a team.  They did an okay job, but almost all of the book's flaws are a result of the fact that they gave themselves unnecessary challenges that they couldn't handle. They're just not good enough writers (yet).  My final, quick criticisms of this book are:  1. Levithan's lower case was pointless and came off as pretentious, just like his character. 2. The ending of the book (the whole last chapter) was unfulfilling, stupid and unrealistic. 3. I thought the character of Jane was superfluous. 4. It's rarely a good idea to have current cultural references in a novel. This thing will be outdated in a couple of years. 5. I know it's petty, but the title is awful. I would have picked up this book when it first came out if it weren't for the title. 6. This is a popcorn book with themes that are very thin and thrown right in your face. Luckily, the non-serious nature of the book made it so that it didn't come off as heavy-handed, but that's also the flaw. It didn't come off as serious or meaningful either. This book lacks something that would make a reader think about it or feel anything after they've closed it. I started another book at the soonest possible moment. This just doesn't stick.  One last note: I was on the phone with my friend last night, and I told him I was reading this book. I described both of the Will Graysons and their general storylines. He immediately guessed which one was written by John Green. Then, he told me things about the characters and story that I hadn't yet told him, saying that &#34;all of his books are that exact same thing.&#34; I haven't read any of John Green's other offerings, so this point did not contribute to my overall starrating, but I did think it was worth bringing up.  Overall: There's no reason not to read this, and I don't necessarily regret spending money on it, but if I were you I would check it out at the library first before purchasing. It's definitely a fast, fun read, but it has glaring flaws.

B0070R8WY2|4.0|When I heard Tramp's magnificent lead single \\"Serpents\\" my expectations for the album were set high. When I saw the album's cover, a tip of the hat to John Cale's eternal [[ASIN:B000006XCU Fear]], the bar went up several notches. Could it be that Ms. Van Etten had really had a breakthrough? To my great disappointment, the answer is \\"not really.\\" While Tramp is a solid set of songs with a number of tracks that have serious staying power, as a whole it doesn't gel.<br /><br />The album starts off unconventionally and defiantly with \\"Warsaw,\\" a short song that runs in circles melodically and lyrically. \\"You, you're something,\\" Sharon sings, and it's not clear whether that's good or bad. It's an interesting opening gambit that seems to work.<br /><br />The song just stops and \\"Give Out\\" strums in. After a dozen listens, I don't know if I like it or not! It has some very emotionally resonant lyrics and images, married to a not very interesting melody. It just repeats itself twice and then ends, and at the end of 4:18 the musical idea is pretty worn out. The weak wordplay of \\"look down/look out\\", \\"hold on/hold out\\", \\"give in/give out\\" works at first, but starts to grate.<br /><br />Serpents is one of the two pillars of the album for me. It's the most essential thing on offer, and shows a strong voice and emotion that are lacking elsewhere. If nothing else, this glorious brutality needs to be in your library.<br /><br />\\"Kevin's\\" was written after giving up smoking and a boyfriend simultaneously. It's gorgeous, but impenetrable.<br /><br />\\"Leonard\\" is the first of two songs that owe too much of their melody to Andrew Bird. It's the lesser offender. \\"I am bad at loving\\" is a sentiment that is underexposed in pop music, but the song just doesn't resonate with me emotionally.<br /><br />My second pillar track is \\"In Line\\", which like Serpents features atmospheric backing vox by Wye Oak's Jenn Wasner. Like Serpents, this song has an original and interesting voice. The stacked vocals after the three minute mark are intoxicating.<br /><br />All I Can tries to rock out a bit, but at this point the midtempo five minute epics have weighed the album down. You can't hate the ever deepening wall of sound on this almost Harrisonian juggernaut, but it doesn't stand out the way it would if there were more variation in song length and tempo.<br /><br />We Are Fine finally changes the sonic palette enough to make the listener sit up. Uke (?) and a duet with Zach Condon of Beirut work surprisingly well on a song that could get too wimpy (\\"take my hand and help me not to shake\\"). If it were my album, I think it would be the second single.<br /><br />Magic Chords is my secret favorite song on the album. The loping yet martial percussion, groovy organ, and syncopated choppy vocals all combine beautifully. It's just a shame that it's hidden on the back half of the album and is superficially unremarkable.<br /><br />Andrew Bird ought to get a writing credit on Ask, because the melody and delivery and sound are strikingly reminiscent of him. Is it Cataracts? Natural Disaster? Spare-Ohs? I can't put my finger on it, but it distracts me.<br /><br />\\"I'm Wrong\\" is very pretty and builds up impressively but it doesn't really do much for me.<br /><br />As a closer Joke or a Lie, in the vein of Wye Oak's Doubt, is incisive but is simultaneously too slow and quiet to close out such a slow and quiet back half of the album AND too busy to be arresting the way Doubt was.<br /><br />Tramp is a hard album to rate. I don't hate a single song, in fact really like more than half, but listening to it as a whole almost puts me to sleep. Partly it's that, despite how similar most tracks sound, there's not a single recognizable writing voice that links the songs. Partly it's that each song has so much STUFF, instrumental and otherwise, crammed into it. Silence in music is a powerful thing that this album could use more of. Partly the emotions on offer all fall into a certainnarrow range. More variation in tempo and more aggressive editing would help the album's listenability as well. I'd call it a three and a half star album.<br /><br />I hope in the future Sharon feels enabled to experiment more with different emotions, colors, instruments, tempos, and topics. Leaving the confessional songwriting mold might be worth a try too. In fact, I can suggest a great album to consider as an example of disparate parts making a unified and excellent whole: John Cale's 'Fear.' I'll be waiting.<br /><br />EDIT: Well, I'm sufficiently stuck on this album that I can't honestly give it only three stars. However, I still feel it's a bit of a mess. (I did sound too harsh on Ask above - it's a great song.)<br /><br />I figured out a way to listen to the album that is much more satisfying to me. I cut out \\"Kevin's\\" and \\"I'm Wrong\\" and reordered the tracks to group the \\"Jenn Wasner songs\\", the \\"Zach Condon songs\\", and the \\"Andrew Bird-esque\\" songs, and it feels so much more natural and listenable. At 38:20, it's closer to my ideal album length of 40 minutes, too. See the altered tracklist in the comments, and give it a try - you may just get more out of it.narrow range. I really enjoyed the book and was flipping pages rapidly, but Baldacci killed it in the last 50 pages when he unloaded one unbelievable plot device after another.<br /><br /> A few minutes after I closed the book, I looked back on it and asked myself why I read it all. The author had destroyed my faith in what started out as a good story, even if it felt like someone retelling a Jack Reacher (Lee Child) adventure.<br /><br /> Warrant Officer John Puller, Jr., Criminal Investigation Division, United States Army is assigned by his superior to investigate a multiple homicide in the tiny hamlet of Drake, West Virginia, in the heart of coal country. Four victims have been executed, one of them being a US Army Colonel who plays a part in preparing the secret briefings for the Army's top brass.<br /><br /> Puller alone is assigned - no partners, no back-ups, no onsite support. To Baldacci's credit, he make this outlandish scenario fly. He has Puller teaming up with local Sherriff's Deputy Sam(antha) Cole. More bodies, three of them, are quickly discovered.<br /><br /> It is only afterwards that you realize just how silly this plot is - and how much it parodies Lee Child. In any event, Baldacci carries us through meeting his own dysfunctional family and then Sam Cole's dysfunctional family and a few other dysfunctional families as well, just to add to the local color.<br /><br /> Puller is a well thought out \\"strong, silent\\" hero type. Baldacci does a solid job of establishing him in the reader's mind. I personally hope to see him again in future Baldacci works with hopefully stronger and more coherent storylines.<br /><br /> The other characters, as one would expect, are painted in lighter brushstrokes, although all the characters are reasonably believable.<br /><br /> There is some relatively minor action along the way, but no floods of blood. \\"Zero Day\\". In many ways, is more a police procedural, then a thrillr.<br /><br /> Anyway, I don't want to spoil this for anyone by getting too detailed.<br /><br /> As I said, Baldacci is an excellent writer and he keeps the story moving and caused me to go to sleep much later than usual for two nights running.<br /><br />But the climax of the story is just plain weird. It left me with the feeling that with all the effort that went into plotting the earlier pages, Balducci just ran into a wall.<br /><br /> Is it worth reading? Yes, I think so. But be prepared to be disappointed in the ending.<br /><br />Jerrya police procedural, then a thrillr.   Anyway, I don't want to spoil this for anyone by getting too detailed. B004Y1USV2|5.0|Probably because they're a group that's been knocking about for decades, Yes has gone through many transitions.  The first was the \\"Try this to get famous\\" stage, which was their first two albums, and then there was the classic prog-rock lineup that kinda started at the Yes Album, and ended with Tormato.  There was the short-lived Downes / Horn version of Yes that put out Drama, and then disbanded.  What resurrected was a very pop-friendly Yes with Rabin in control (though Trevor Horn produced their Grammy-winning album) which lasted a few albums.  What followed after was a confused group that put out a lot of sporatic stuff...which I can't complain too much about because I LOVED Magnification.<br /><br />So Jon Anderson can't tour, the group decides to go on with a replacement singer.  Ok.  Not sure how that's going to play out.  Anderson is one of the cornerstones of the group.<br /><br />Well, in Fly From Here Benoit ends up becoming a Trevor Horn clone. I liked most incarnations of Yes, though the Trevor Rabin years I found a bit tiresome.  However, I really liked Drama, and thus I like this album...a lot.  Some very catchy tunes, some drawn-out works, and lots of variety.  In my opinion, while the classic Yes years has their variations, the music presented here has a certain dream-like, fantastic quality to it; more subtle than their other works.<br /><br />If you're one of the people who look for the classic lineup, this probably isn't the album for you.  If you liked Drama, you'll definately like this album.

I'd love to give this film a 5 star rating, I really would, but I just can't bring myself to do it. Sure it has all of the elements that make for a great Sci Fi action movie, which is one of my favorite movie categories ever. It's got great space battles, cool spaceships, weird aliens, exciting action sequences, and some of the greatest characters that ever graced TV and movie screens, Captain James T. Kirk, Spock, Bones McCoy, Scotty and all of the rest. I practically grew up with Star Trek and have loved it since I can remember--But, this movie just has way too many flaws that are hard to ignore.<br /><br />Firstly, I don't really have much of a problem with the cast, I think that for the most part they picked some pretty decent actors to represent the U.S.S. Enterprise crew. My problem with it mostly is that while the makers of this film had a good and noble idea to try and make Star Trek more exciting to modern day viewers by making it more action-packed like a Star Wars movie, BUT, they ended up copying just a little bit too much from Star Wars for my liking. You've heard it all before I'm sure and I don't want to drag down on this review with a lot of rehashed complaints that other reviewers have already exponentionally expounded on but, [Spoiler Alert!] the scene where Kirk gets exiled on the frozen planet Delta Vega, I mean, come on...I was thinking at the back of my mind about Luke Skywalker on Hoth all the way during this scene. But, then again, what other kind of planet surface haven't the Star Wars films already been featured prominently on? Abrams movie, Nero, without all of the cool facial tattoos of course. Hmmm? Facial tattoos...Darth Maul anyone? I mean, Come on J.J., did you even watch Star Trek Nemesis? 0312641893|5.0|4.5 stars. Cinder is retelling of the classic Cinderella story, but with a fantastic sci-fi twist. Since I love retellings, I've been looking forward to this one and it was even better than I expected!<br /><br />Kudos to Marissa Meyer for making our futuristic Cinderella a cyborg which may sound weird, but it totally works for this book. The novel is set in New Beijing, a city rebuilt after the fourth world war. The world building is awesome and when I read about the setting of New Beijing, I could not help but think of scenes from one of my all time favorite sci-fi movies, Blade Runner. While Cinder is not dark like Blade Runner, there are the hovercrafts, Asian influences and androids that remind me of Cinder.<br /><br />A big change from the original fairytale is instead of one villain, there are two. Adri is Cinder's stepmother but she is really the wife of the man who adopted Cinder. He died shortly after the adoption and Adri has hated Cinder ever since. Adri is bitter, petty and does her best to put Cinder down. While we hate Adri for bullying Cinder, the real threat is from Queen Levana, ruler of a race of people with paranormal abilities who inhabit the moon. Levana is evil, manipulative and cunning with plans to rule Earth, but she needs to marry Prince Kai to put her plans into motion.<br /><br />Cinder is a very likable character. She has a bad home situation that she desperately wants to escape from, but of course, her destiny is more entwined with Prince Kai and the Lunars than she can ever imagine. There is more to Cinder than meets the eye and it is easy to figure out very early in the novel. With Adri's verbal abuse and Cinder's insecurities over her cyborg enhancements, it's easy to empathize with her, especially when Prince Kai flirts with her and Cinder thinks she is not good enough for him.<br /><br />I don't feel that I got to know Prince Kai well enough in this book. A few portions of the novel were told from his perspective and we see that he is a nice guy who puts the safety and well being of his people before his own happiness. He is sarcastic, smart and completely swoon worthy. His friendship with Cinder is sweet and leaves room to grow and develop. I really hope we get a lot more of Kai in upcoming books because he has the potential to become one of my favorite book boys.<br /><br />Cinder does not end with a cliffhanger but leaves you wanting so much more! Cinder is an awesome debut novel and I hate that I have to wait until next year to read the sequel. 0955153077|5.0|Received this book this week and I must say I am thoroughly happy with it. As I expected from the preview I've seen on 3DTotalPublishing's site, this book is not only intended for those just beginning to learn about painting in Photoshop, but instead presents rendering techniques valuable even for professional artists, like some of the artists presenting this book state(for example Kekai Kotaki from ArenaNet's \\"Guild Wars\\" and Daarken). Still, if you're looking for a more images-filled type of book this is not the one for you, since it goes in-depth providing a lot of written information and just the necessary amount of imagery. Each chapter of the main parts is written and illustrated by an artist who presents his own approach, but following the matter of study. You will also be provided with a reminder inside the book to download the accompanying resources for the tutorials in this book from 3DTotalPublishing's site(there you can also see a small format full preview for this book)<br /><br />The book starts off with the basics: presenting the Photoshop workspace, optimizing it for your own needs and in accordance to your computer's resources, introducing the main features such as working with colors, brushes, textures, blending modes, how to establish composition, perspective and basic tonal values, using photographs for texturing or as matte painting bases, working with masks, wand tool, filters and more. Great insight for beginners and medium-level users of PS.<br /><br />It continues with art fundamentals, where you will find a more in-depth presentation of what makes a painting good or great. Lighting, value, color, composition, perspective and depth, portraying emotions in your character or landscape and even storytelling. You will learn about the Fibonacci number and his \\"golden ratio\\", different types of perspective and much more. A very insightful chapter with extended text supported by just the right amount of images, as with the rest of this book.<br /><br />A \\"complete project\\" chapter follows then, basically presenting you with the creation of a finished piece of digital painting using all the info previously presented in the book. Take it as a revision of some sorts, combined with an in-depth, step-by-step tutorial. Great for evaluating your own learning speed and process.<br /><br />The book goes on to hands down type of practice, showing you how to create speed paintings dealing with SF character and landscapes, paint your piece using only tonal values of grays and only afterwards apply the color glazes, how to create a finished piece using elements of photos and combining them with digital painting - a technique known as matte painting, widely used in feature films' concept design -, and finally how to tackle fantasy landscapes, in this case an eery skull-canyon-like view overlooking a river and waterfall. Starting with this chapter and continuing until further you will probably see how the pages at which you find certain chapters don't coincide anymore with the pages given in the Contents page. Or at least that's how it is in my own copy's case.<br /><br />The \\"Project overviews\\" chapter deals once again with step-by-step processes of different artists' finished pieces. Again the emphasis is more on text, but you get a decent amount of images also. Personally I appreciate the written part more, since it allows one to use the given techniques in his or her own way, without the pressure to make the final piece as likely in style as the artist's work.<br /><br />\\"Quick tips\\" is an extremely well-made chapter in my opinion, presenting you with short and concise \\"how to\\" approaches on different tricky elements, such as trees, sandstorms, smoke, underwater, skin, make something look wet, all those detail things which make a painting come alive.<br /><br />And finally the \\"Breakdown gallery\\" is the major eye-candy part of this book for those who were looking forward to it, you basically get step-by-step kind of tutorials containing only 5 images each - including the finished artwork -, so it's up to you to pay attention and observe since you don't get any text accompanying them. However, after a book packed full of text and information, you should be able to analyse and decipher what and how each artist used in every step in order to achieve the final result.<br /><br />And that's about it. All in all a great book which offers you all the necessary information without cluttering too many pictures, allowing you to use the given tips in your own way and creating your own image, not just style-copies. Definitely worth its money and then some.

0316017930|4.0|Outliers, written by Malcolm Gladwell, is a story about success. While the story goes on, Malcolm explains and gives several examples of how success is achieved by several different individuals. There isn't just one way that this success is met by these select people, and that is what makes this book so interesting. He defines these successful people as Outliers, which in the beginning of the book is defined as, \\"something that is situated away from or classed differently from a main or related body.\\" These people have achieved such a high level of success, that they are considered \\"different\\" than the rest. While exploring the different scenarios, Gladwell uses great detail, and a lot of explanation on the topic he is discussing, so there is never confusion. Each chapter is about something different, so it keeps the reader interested instead of just sticking to one topic throughout the whole book. Gladwell uses some history to help explain the topics, so the reader has more background knowledge on the topic rather than just knowing that people were very successful. His use of words were another thing that stood out to me. He didn't use big words, and when there was something that needed to be expanded on, he expanded in italics at the end of the page. Overall, Outliers is a great book, and I recommend it to everyone!

0316017930|5.0|Many people think they know what it takes to be successful; however, in his book Outliers, Malcolm Gladwell's unique writing style and use of many different examples gives us an idea about normally overlooked factors of success.  This book is a must-read if you want to know more about what it takes to be successful.  As it turns out, there are a lot of hidden things that aren't common knowledge, such as family background, opportunities, and the 10,000 hour rule.  Gladwell does a great job of explaining all of them and provides his ideas with substantial facts and evidence to support his ideas.  If you think that being a genius automatically makes someone successful, you better think again.  You don't necessarily have to be super smart in order to become successful someday; you also need other skills, opportunities, luck, or all of them together.  This book will keep you thinking as you read about somewhat quirky things about outliers in the world of hockey players, computer whizzes, lawyers, pilots, and more.  Some of the people Gladwell discusses are well known, such as Bill Gates and the Beatles.  After hearing about many other people, you'll also hear the story about Gladwell's family history, which includes the overlooked factors of success he discusses in the book.  Overall, he did a great job of writing this book.  He ties it all together extremely well at the end by once again referencing all the outliers he talked about throughout it.  I would definitely read it again someday.

0307352145|5.0|Albert Einstein was one of the more famous \\"introverts\\" who claimed, \\"Its not that I am so smart, its that I stay with the problem longer.\\"  Cain uses examples like this to highlight the inner workings and social contributions of the introvert type. In this case, introverts she explains are often better suited at quiet reflection, deep thinking and analytical problem solving.  Sufficient to say, this is not a book extolling the virtues of extroverts (who may squirm reading parts).  It is a rugged defense and forthright advocacy of the essential and irreplaceable roles, value and contributions of the introvert type.  This is a smart, well-researched and meaty book on the popular psychology topic of introverts compared to extroverts.  As such, it is less a simple \\"self-help\\" format and more investigative distillation of the various studies on the topic.  However, the research is presented in a casual, easy-to-read way that should not alienate or put off too many.  Its easy to see a next book by the same author - one less in scope but focused on applying the many powerful conclusions Cain's research for introvert personality types to gain new awareness and empowerment to shed any inferiority and use their powerful gifts to change the world.  Part of this research involves the latest findings of fMRI brain imaging.  Some of the conclusions are useful to the lay person, but the same person might roll their eyes with too many words like \\"orbitofrontal cortex,\\" \\"nucleas accumbum\\" and \\"amygdala\\" (regions of the brain) or sentences like \\"So where was his neocortex when he was chasing the stock market gains?\\"  Cain does not hesitate to apply such conclusions to take direct aim at \\"forceful extroverts\\" who took insane, excessive financial risks and nearly brought the financial world to near-disaster (while belittling the many introvert colleagues who warned them). She backs up such claims with key research points.  Parts I found of most interesting were:  - her fleshing out ofvarious definitions of extrovert vs. introvert (no one is 100% of either)  - her sub-division of highly sensitive individuals (HSI) and how they are so much more reactive to stimuli than extroverts  - how introverts are threat-sensitive oriented vs. the reward-driven orientation of extroverts  - the comparison of Asian values (largely introvert traits of listening first, quiet reflection, group moral dynamics) vs. American values (extrovert-dominated values like self-promotion, passionate communication and drive for achievement...).  Yet, she does a decent job to avoid stereotypes to get valid and useful insights across.  This book should have a serious place with professions like counselors, human resource professionals, educators, parents of super-shy kids and anyone identifying as an introvert.  At the end of the matter, author Cain makes a case for balance between action ... and reflection as she gives a new voice to the often ignored, less respected introverts of the world who should gain new insights and empowerment from Cain's solid and fresh contribution to the topic. I'll start with the characters -- Lena specifically.<br /><br />Lena is a type of character that I love reading, you know where the character arc is probably going to go, however you don't necessarily know how they're going to go from \\"Point A\\" to \\"Point B.\\"  She starts out as an advocate for the procedure, and from the very beginning you think her best friend, Hana, is going to be a catalyst for change... but if anything Hana's feelings make Lena retract into herself and cling harder to what she's been brought up to believe.  It's this desire to cling to societal standards that solidified my thoughts on the fact that she'd be an interesting character to travel with.  Watching her morph from this one, rather \\"safe\\" character to this strong and independent character was fun -- the transformation also felt natural, and I think that's important.<br /><br />As for our male lead, Alex, I really enjoyed him. 0761169253|5.0|If you've been paying attention to certain parts of the Web recently, you may be familiar with a rising chorus of voices talking about creativity as \\"remixing.\\"  The broad thesis is that creativity isn't a mystical flash of insight in the mind of a lone genius, but rather a combinatorial, collaborative process in which artists and designers consciously and gradually combine existing ideas into novel forms.  Books like \\"The Gift\\", by Lewis Hyde, and video series like \\"Everything is a Remix\\", by Kirby Ferguson, champion this burgeoning idea (and are name-checked by Kleon at the end of \\"Artist\\").  But if Hyde and Ferguson are the theorists charting the contours of these new ideas, Kleon is the practitioner, the man-of-artistic-action, bringing the means and the message to the people.<br /><br />\\"Steal Like An Artist\\" began as a lecture given by Kleon at Broome Community College that later emerged as a viral blog post.  Kleon makes no effort to hide the fact that the blog post forms the skeleton of \\"Artist\\".  But even if you've memorized the post, Kleon layers enough muscle and flesh on it that you feel like you're encountering his core ideas all over again.  The same rush of discovery and energy awaits.<br /><br />Kleon describes himself as \\"a writer that draws,\\" and \\"Artist\\" is proof of that.  He designed the book himself and his voice and style shine through.  \\"Artist\\" feels unified, innovative, balanced, and, above all, intimate.  The book is small, like a big cocktail napkin.  It's full of illustrations by Kleon and little flourishes that keep things brisk as you read.  The small size makes the book feel approachable, ready to provide a quick inspiration burst if need be.  Kleon describes ten basic principles to boost your creativity.  He lists them on the back cover of the book (a choice that Kindle purchasers will miss) so that they're easily referenced.  It's a small touch, but emblematic of the book's careful construction.<br /><br />Most importantly, \\"Artist\\"is focused on practicality.  Kleon has absorbed the lessons of Hyde and Ferguson, but he wants to do more than evangelize; he wants to transform.  \\"Artist\\" is stuffed with practical tips that you can adopt.  In fact, there's a section at the very end of the book titled \\"What Now?\\", in which Kleon gives you a long, itemized list of things you can do *right now* to prime your creative pump.<br /><br />One recent book that Kleon doesn't reference is \\"Where Good Ideas Come From\\", by Steve Johnson.  \\"Good Ideas\\" is one of the best new books about spurring creativity, but it's primarily focused on principles of creativity and their historical origins.  \\"Artist\\" is a perfect companion to \\"Good Ideas\\".  Once you've read Johnson's book and your head is full of theory, Kleon's book comes along and gives you a good, firm (and lighthearted) kick in the pants to send you on your way.  You certainly don't need to read \\"Good Ideas\\" or any other book to receive the full benefit of \\"Artist\\".  It's a short, heady blast of exuberance that's guaranteed to kick-start your imagination.

Nothing earth-shattering, but I can easily imagine this finding success on the radio. The chorus strives for the same rousing singalong nature of We Are Young. It doesn't quite get there, mostly because the song's more subdued, but it's close. 9/10  5. It Gets Better - Back to uptempo. The production here's very interesting. Frantic, cluttered and a cool mix of electronic and acoustic. The verses don't do much for me, but I love the chorus. 8/10  6. Why Am I The One - The album's best straight-up ballad. Though it takes awhile to get to the hook, it's worth it because the harmonies are simply gorgeous. It has a real throwback sound in the best way. It sounds like a lost AOR hit from the 70's. 10/10  7. All Alone - The album's first real foray into hip-hop influenced production. There's a reggae vibe too. In fact, the entire track is a hodgepodge of sounds that shouldn't necessarily go together. And yet it all works. 9/10  8. All Alright - This is where the album starts to sag for me. This certainly isn't bad, but the melody and production just kind of plods along. It's heading for epic territory, but I'm not sure it gets there. Missing some oomph, maybe. 7/10  9. One Foot - This is a bit more experimental. Over a spare, (very) repetitive brassy beat, this song is much more \\"chanty\\" (yeah, I made up that word) than melodic. In other words, it's an experiment that I'm not sure entirely works. 7/10  10. Stars - My least favorite track. Personally, I find that the high vocal parts grate and I really don't like the vocoder/autotune choices. I realize it's intentional, but this just seems to meander along. And at nearly seven minutes, that's a lot of meandering. Honestly, take it off the album and you've got a much better listen. 5/10  11. Out On The Town - I can't believe this is only a bonus track, because it's as good or better than half of the songs on the actual album. It's not necessarily the most memorable of the bunch, but its solid pop songcraft is a welcome sound after that last track. 8/10  Album Grade: 8.3/10

B005599XJK|5.0|No lover in the world ever wrote a valentine more exquisite than Virginia Woolf's tribute to her lover Vita Sackville-West, \\"Orlando.\\"<br /><br />And few movie adaptations are as coyly, exquisitely lovely as the 1992 movie adapted from that book, a magical-realism tale about a perpetually youthful, charming hero/ine who traverses three centuries and both genders. Tilda Swinton has the right combination of androgyny and intelligence to perfectly embody Orlando, and director Sally Potter gilds and perfumes every set and costume.<br /><br />Orlando (Swinton) was born a young aristocratic man in the time of Queen Elizabeth I, and when the dying monarch visited his home she became his new court favorite. She also bid him, \\"Do not fade, do not wither, do not grow old.\\"<br /><br />And Orlando did as she said. With the death of the queen and his father, Orlando's passionate, curious personality attracted many women -- and during the Great Freeze he fell in love with Sasha, a mercurial Russian princess (Charlotte Valandrey) who enthralled him, but left him as he ice began to thaw. Bereft of true love, he devoted himself to poetry and entertainment.<br /><br />But then he's assigned to be an ambassador to Constantinople, and something strange happens -- while a bloody revolution rages, he sleeps for a full week... and wakes newly metamorphosed into a woman. With the same mind and soul but a female body, Orlando sets out on a new life of poetry (befriending Pope!), sex and legality, stretching all the way to the twentieth century -- when she finally finds peace.<br /><br />\\"Orlando\\" is a treat for the senses, filled with showers of gold dust, luxuriant flowers, pale sunlight, golden sands, cities veiled in ice, dark rivers, snowy forests and mist-filled hedge mazes. It feels like Sally Potter took Woolf's beautiful book and sprinkled it with roses, gold and crystals -- and it just adds a suitably magical atmosphere to a already unreal story.<br /><br />The center of all this is Swinton, who plays Orlando in both incarnations, and she's utterly brilliant. Her androgynous features and slim body mean she can pass for both a man and a woman, and she manages to carry both genders off beautifully -- she captures gangly boyish grace, sleek femininity, and a sort of chummy male attitude with equal skill.<br /><br />And she captures Orlando's elusive personality. Her Orlando is all puckish charm, sweetness and unleashed passion -- even to the audience. While watching a street performance of \\"Othello,\\" he glances at the camera and whispers, \\"Terrific play!\\", as if we're trailing after him over the centuries.<br /><br />And as I mentioned, Potter does a brilliant job with a very difficult book, sticking faithfully to most of Woolf's novel and adding a shimmering, silken atmosphere to it. There are lots of beautiful scenes that could have stepped out of a Pre-Raphaelite painting, and she deftly handles the passing centuries by having Orlando sprint through walls of mist. The ending is slightly different from Woolf's novel, but it has the same pleasantly timeless, thoughtful quality -- the only downside is that weird freaky angel thing. WHAT was that about?<br /><br />Fortunately, the freaky angel thing is the one downside in \\"Orlando,\\" a timelessly sensual movie that perfectly highlights Tilda Swinton. I see where some other reviewers think the book and characters are a bit stale.  Maybe it's because I haven't read the other books in the series, but I found the characters fascinating.  Such is the author's skill that you feel you are really in the period, and the characters' troubles are treated realistically and with less dime-novel heroics than Cornwell employed in the Sharpe books.  The characterization and structure overall are outstanding. I think this is one of Cornwell's best.

B003E88TKS|5.0|While I'd hardly be inclined to label this movie as a must-see for everyone, it does work on several levels so as to appeal to a rather wide audience, making it certainly worth most people's while.<br /><br />I suppose the most recognizable piece of the film is its star, George Clooney. No suspension of reality is required in order to believe the character Clooney is playing; he fits the part real well. So well in fact, that the emotional evocation brought out by his character, Ryan Bingham feels (dare I say it?) real and far from fabricated (though I figure it was, all the more credit to dir. Jason Reitman). A good-looking, globe-trotting bachelor, who has sworn off marriage and any chance at a stable home, Clooney's emotional development throughout the course of the film is what to watch for.<br /><br />So, forget the planes, hotels and frequent flyer miles; more than any of that, the movie is about a man's solitude (brought about at his own behest) and the loneliness that so often follows when one thinks that they can take on the world all on their own. The difference between being surrounded by people (as Clooney often protests) and actually being with other people, is one that takes the course of the film to discover. While the entire film builds upon this idea, I felt it most evident during the wedding sequence, where Clooney's character returns home almost a stranger. More specifically, there is a scene where Clooney asks his sister if she wants him to walk her down the aisle (evidently after the passing of their father). She informs him she already has someone else. The look on Clooney's face and his subsequent stumbling for words is perhaps the most memorable part the film, at least emotionally. I mean it when I say, you feel his character's pain, and that's quite the accomplishment considering the type of character that he is.<br /><br />Of course I could go on spilling praise for the rest of the cast, they all worked well, but it really is just Clooney's picture. A film about interconnectedness, isolation, and individualism in the modern world; one can't help leaving the film without thinking that maybe life's better with feet firmly on the ground.nectedness, isolation, and individualism in the modern world; one can't help leaving the film without thinking that maybe life's better with feet firmly on the ground.

The only problem was that the album- and tour that followed it- also became the thing which tore them apart. Shortly after the exit of lead singer David Lee Roth, Sammy Hagar stepped in to fill the role for the next ten years with the band. When tensions boiled over in 1996, Hagar left the band and Van Halen were reunited with original singer Roth- but only for a short minute at the MTV Video Music Awards. The band then recruited yet another lead singer- this time Gary Cherone of the band Extreme for their overtly political album \\"Van Halen III\\". The album floundered and Cherone departed soon after. Over the next decade, the band experienced numerous shakeups- such as getting dropped from their longtime label Warner Bros. Records and the departure of longtime bassist Michael Anthony in 2006. Eddie's son Wolfgang took over for Anthony and soon after David Lee Roth was invited to rejoin the band after a 25 year absence.<br /><br />In 2012, they released their first album on their new label Interscope Records- \\"A Different Kind Of Truth\\". On this new recording, they enlist the production talent of John Shanks- who is best known for his work with Ashlee Simpson, Sheryl Crow, Alanis Morrisette and Michelle Branch. While Van Halen have a pop music producer behind their new record, the album is anything but pop music. In fact, this album sounds exactly like a Van Halen album- and a great one at that. They open with the first single, a song about the appeciation of body artwork \\"Tattoo\\" and sing about a one night stand on \\"She's The Woman\\". They go back to their thrash roots on seedy underworld themed \\"China Town\\" and on \\"As Is\\". They sing about getting rid of a moody woman on \\"You And Your Blues\\" and pay tribute to those who rose from their bad situations on \\"Blood And Fire\\". They tackle the subject of procrastination on \\"The Trouble With Never\\" and seek spirituality on the bluesy rocker \\"Stay Frosty\\". Overall, \\"A Different Kind Of Truth\\" is a fantastic record from Van Halen and could possibly be a strong contender for the best album of the year. Despite all they have been through within the last fifteen years, it's nice to hear from them again.n the bluesy rocker \\"Stay Frosty\\".
